#### Teaching Stories
>[!QUOTE]

>[!QUOTE]  
>One caveat for using stories to teach: remember, it only works about 70 percent of the time. When you inspire and encourage people to think for themselves, they are more actively engaged and pay better attention. However, at times, their interpretation will not be what you expected. Thatâ€™s the price you pay for creative engagement. ([LocationÂ 1076](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1076)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655686}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Find people who have the skills you want. Study their stories, and tell their stories. We tend to teach what we need to learn, and I canâ€™t think of a better way to do it. ([LocationÂ 1157](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1157)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655687}


---
#### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Storytelling brings substance to any vision process. When you apply the discipline of interpreting your vision with a story, the process often exposes â€œplot holesâ€ that may send you back to the drawing board. Building a Vision story is a good way to run thought experiments that can anticipate contrary perceptions or unintended outcomes. ([LocationÂ 1207](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1207)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655689}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our purpose here is to build a future story that pulls us in. A â€œpullâ€ story conjures positive emotions such as desire, hope, belonging, or happiness. ([LocationÂ 1228](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1228)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655690}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Stories based on negative emotions such as fear (greed is just another word for fear) fuel stress (also a word for fear) and feed perceptions of danger, scarcity, and us/them thinking. Fear is a physiological and psychological state that narrows vision and limits creativity. ([LocationÂ 1229](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1229)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655691}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The molecules of fear sacrifice peripheral vision in favor of focusing on problems and our three basic human responses: fight, flight, or freeze. Fear can make you stupid. It compartmentalizes every IQ point you have into closed loops of worst-case scenarios. ([LocationÂ 1231](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1231)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655692}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? Start by imagining a time 5 to 10 years from now when you have just achieved a set of tangible goals (experiment with a few or conduct a vision trip for each goal one at a time). Let your imagination observe a typical day in tangible detail. You may have to wait, but your imagination will do its job if you are patient. ([LocationÂ 1239](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1239)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655693}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>You can illustrate your Vision story like a comic strip or find an analogy that is similar to the achievements, obstacles, and events you imagined. ([LocationÂ 1245](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1245)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655694}

>>[!NOTE]  
>>Write fiction, past tense as if itd already happened

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story carved in stone is not as good as one that is loose enough to adapt and change along the way. ([LocationÂ 1271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655695}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>One final note: In my humble opinion, fiction is a dangerous place to go hunting for Vision stories because even great fiction is an invented world that may have been idealized and was certainly manipulated to grab and keep attention. The benefit of true stories is that they actually happened (at least once), so it seems more likely that you could make them happen again. ([LocationÂ 1338](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1338)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655696}

#### Value-in-Action Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>JUST ABOUT EVERY story you tell about a disaster with a cable company, plumber, electrician, or other contractor could be considered a Value-in-Action story. ([LocationÂ 1348](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1348)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522250}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Value-in-Action stories build either inspiration, creativity and innovation, or frustration and apathy. ([LocationÂ 1364](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1364)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522251}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Metaphors are ministories that help us frame complexity into a familiar package. We use the war metaphor a lot: the war on AIDS, for instance. ([LocationÂ 1369](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1369)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522252}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Each culture prizes different values. Mix two cultures and you better start sharing Value-in-Action stories. { #ref-552522253}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Like many values, for instance, trust is not strictly rational. Trust means I can fall and you wonâ€™t leave me. Trust means that if I sacrifice for your good, you will return the favor in the future. Trust rates good intentions over current results and allows for second chances. ([LocationÂ 1438](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1438)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522254}

#### I-Know-What-You-Are-Thinking Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>Many arguments are fueled less by the â€œneed to be rightâ€ and more by the chronically unmet need to be heard and respected. Thatâ€™s where I-Know-What-You-Are-Thinking stories come into play. { #ref-552522256}

### Perfecting the Craft
#### Sensory Details Make Stories Experiential

#### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. These loops cycle endlessly as new ideas are met with a â€œyes, butâ€ rejection followed by sympathy, which elicits more examples of feeling trapped, which trigger new ideas for change, and you are back to â€œyes, but.â€ Itâ€™s an infinite loop that you can focus like a laser once you are willing to choose one value over another. ([LocationÂ 1908](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1908)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522266}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The breadth and depth of your investment shines through your story with brightness, intensity, and essence. Itâ€™s like using a 100-watt bulb instead of a 30-watt bulb. Each bulb is the same size, turned on for the same three minutes, but one is more intense and its light reaches farther than the other. ([LocationÂ 1935](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1935)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522267}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>This is a good ratio for us to remember: millions of dollars and hours beyond reason are invested to create one experience. ([LocationÂ 1942](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1942)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522268}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Thatâ€™s brevity at its best. Take your longest story and ask yourself, â€œWhat is the central message I want to communicate?â€ ([LocationÂ 1953](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1953)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522269}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you were going to give a one-hour presentation on your most important project and its relevance to the organizationâ€™s mission, what would it look like? Notice your mental to-do list. Does it start with time to reflect or opening PowerPoint to record a few bullet points? I know one large organization where most presentations easily include an average of 70 PowerPoint slides, called a â€œdeck.â€ They spend hours formatting data and adding animation, images, and graphs. { #ref-552522270}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Brevity is better achieved by turning the computer off and asking yourself the basic questions. Who am I? Why am I here? What is the highest possible outcome of my presentation? This may not feel like real work because it involves a lot of staring into space, going for a walk, or even heading to the gym, but this is the work that builds a cohesive message. { #ref-552522271}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Graphs tidy up reality, whereas stories reveal and help resolve the mess. Avoiding the messiness of reality is not the only way to be brief. Taking a stand achieves clarity, brevity, and the power of a clear, clean message. It requires that you both respect and trust your audience. And the minute you make that decision, it rearranges your presentation into a story that sticks, and it helps you edit your graphs and numbers down to the vital few. ([LocationÂ 1992](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1992)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522272}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Core values always compete at some point. It takes courage to make the call, in advance, about which value you choose at the extremes when two values conflict. ([LocationÂ 1996](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1996)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. Retaining the ambiguity and then testing a story in a low-risk experiment is a much better way to minimize risk in storytelling. ([LocationÂ 2048](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2048)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522275}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. Groups in the unfamiliar territory of images, metaphor, and emotion often seek relief by oversimplifying these immeasurable qualities and converting them into some kind of quantifiable criteria. This default to numbers doesnâ€™t make for a better story; it just allows for an easier decision. ([LocationÂ 2053](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2053)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522276}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In her book On Becoming an Artist, Ellen Langer cites research into the â€œillusion of controlâ€ as she ruthlessly deconstructs our favorite habits of using set principles and cognitive frames to improve decisions.9 Particularly in business, routines that make decisions easier may sacrifice the engaging messiness of human experience. The better we get at acting like automatons, the less meaningful our stories become. ([LocationÂ 2062](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2062)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522277}

#### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. If a middle manager gives a report to senior management, secretly thinking they are a bunch of idiots and that his or her presentation will be futile, chances are the presentation will be futile. These nonverbal communications may never reach the presenterâ€™s or senior managementâ€™s awareness, but negative feelings distort interpretations. ([LocationÂ 2152](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2152)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522279}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. Senior managers may think some workers join unions to exploit that power and compensate for failed careers. School teachers think the principal is really a pawn. The CEO thinks board members are already biased. There are a thousand stories out there that leave you feeling â€œrightâ€ but keep you from being connected. ([LocationÂ 2155](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2155)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522280}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In the same way that no person can experience life without bias, no story is without bias. A story without bias is boring because it becomes disembodied, sterilized, and dehumanized. Behavior is always based on some point of view, so own it and demonstrate that you arenâ€™t blind to (or blinded by) your personal bias. ([LocationÂ 2166](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2166)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522281}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Point of view alters meaning, but, more important to you, point of view also creates meaning. Choosing a time, place, and point of view from which to tell a story is what makes a story feel like a real experience. Using your story to travel through time and space can demonstrate how many equally valid points of view you see. ([LocationÂ 2173](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2173)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522282}

#### Story Listening
>[!QUOTE]  
>Before you make another presentation about a future project, I recommend you stop and listen to the stories that are operating right now in your mind and in your audienceâ€™s minds. This process feels awkward. Asking coworkers out of the blue, â€œTell me a story about hope,â€ is a bit weird. Itâ€™s better if you lay some groundwork. Give them a reasonable basis for the question. For example, say, â€œIâ€™m trying to get a fix on how we approach projects, and I need your help. This doesnâ€™t have to apply to your job, but how would you characterize our willingness to risk in pursuit of extraordinary outcomes?â€ Be prepared to stay silent when they give you strange looks. Steer them away from hypothetical or existential discussions. Keep asking for a concrete example. Ask them for a story. If necessary, tell your own story about a recent or important experience of hope or hopelessness. Donâ€™t force your story or theirs to move in the direction you would preferâ€”that will give you a false reading. Donâ€™t try to craft the story in any way. This is a process of mapping the mental terrain. You donâ€™t draw maps based on where you wish the mountains were. Useful maps show where the mountains actually are. ([LocationÂ 2227](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2227)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522284}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Holding your story at bay while you fully experience othersâ€™ points of view without judgments is the key to real listening. ([LocationÂ 2270](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2270)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522285}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our world moves so fast that we are pressured to make sense of incoming data as quickly as possible. Speed and focus have become unquestioned values applied to communication. People mindlessly try to improve communication by increasing speed and focus. Thatâ€™s great when you seek only to inform. However, when you seek to influence, speed and focus narrow the bandwidth across which you communicate. Narrow connections produce less influence. Listening is slow and wide rather than fast and narrow. ([LocationÂ 2271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522286}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Listening is not something you do all the time or without end. It is a choice based on what you can process and how important it is for you to make a connection. Itâ€™s not like handing the keys to your brain over to a stranger. You are still in charge. True, genuine listening feels risky when time is short, but you donâ€™t abdicate decision making just because you slow down long enough to imagine another personâ€™s experiences. Listening may take you to a place you donâ€™t want to go. Listening may reveal trouble about which you were blissfully ignorant. Still, listening always makes you smarter and earns you stronger connections to work with and through. ([LocationÂ 2301](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2301)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522287}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you donâ€™t have a high tolerance for imperfection, people will shy away from telling you the â€œwhole story.â€ And itâ€™s common wisdom that the rest of the story is where you find the most interesting bits. One time, I heard someone complain that her story was boring, so I automatically replied, â€œThen you need to tell the truth.â€ She responded, â€œDo you mean the part when I had an affair with the minister?â€ That certainly didnâ€™t sound very boring to me. { #ref-552522288}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Borrowing Genius ([LocationÂ 2327](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2327)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522289}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Design engineers also use templates to describe requirements in the terms of a user story: â€œAs a (type of user) I want (goal) so that (reason why).â€ While these user stories are currently very technical, notice that this template actually asks for three of the six stories addressed in this book, â€œWho are you? ##### Story Thinking Is Not the Opposite of Objective Thinking
##### Relax Your Internal Critic

#### Story Is Experience Reconstituted
>[!QUOTE]  
>The best you can do is bring these transformational experiences to life with a story that is so vivid, so impactful, it feels as if the listeners were actually there. For them, a story becomes a type of personal experience, even if it is experienced vicariously. This is why I define story as a reimagined experience narrated with enough detail and feeling to cause your listenersâ€™ imaginations to experience it as real. #### Teaching Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>AT ITS BEST, a Teaching story transports listeners into an experience that empowers them to emotionally feel as well as hear, see, taste, touch, and smell an excellent performance or the unforgettable consequences of a poor performance. It demonstrates how new behaviors create new results. It is a no-risk demonstration: a trial by imagination. { #ref-542655685}

Thatâ€™s the price you pay for creative engagement. ([LocationÂ 1076](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1076)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Storytelling brings substance to any vision process. When you apply the discipline of interpreting your vision with a story, the process often exposes â€œplot holesâ€ that may send you back to the drawing board. Building a Vision story is a good way to run thought experiments that can anticipate contrary perceptions or unintended outcomes. ([LocationÂ 1207](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1207)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655689}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our purpose here is to build a future story that pulls us in. A â€œpullâ€ story conjures positive emotions such as desire, hope, belonging, or happiness. ([LocationÂ 1228](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1228)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655690}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Stories based on negative emotions such as fear (greed is just another word for fear) fuel stress (also a word for fear) and feed perceptions of danger, scarcity, and us/them thinking. Fear is a physiological and psychological state that narrows vision and limits creativity. ([LocationÂ 1229](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1229)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655691}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The molecules of fear sacrifice peripheral vision in favor of focusing on problems and our three basic human responses: fight, flight, or freeze. Fear can make you stupid. It compartmentalizes every IQ point you have into closed loops of worst-case scenarios. ([LocationÂ 1231](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1231)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655692}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? Start by imagining a time 5 to 10 years from now when you have just achieved a set of tangible goals (experiment with a few or conduct a vision trip for each goal one at a time). Let your imagination observe a typical day in tangible detail. You may have to wait, but your imagination will do its job if you are patient. ([LocationÂ 1239](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1239)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655693}


---

{ #ref-542655694}

>>Write fiction, past tense as if itd already happened

#### Value-in-Action Stories
>[!QUOTE]

#### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. These loops cycle endlessly as new ideas are met with a â€œyes, butâ€ rejection followed by sympathy, which elicits more examples of feeling trapped, which trigger new ideas for change, and you are back to â€œyes, but.â€ Itâ€™s an infinite loop that you can focus like a laser once you are willing to choose one value over another. >[!QUOTE]

>[!QUOTE]  
>Graphs tidy up reality, whereas stories reveal and help resolve the mess. Avoiding the messiness of reality is not the only way to be brief. #### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. ([LocationÂ 2048](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2048)) #âœ‚ï¸

>[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. #### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. >[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. >[!QUOTE]

#### Story Listening
>[!QUOTE]  
>Before you make another presentation about a future project, I recommend you stop and listen to the stories that are operating right now in your mind and in your audienceâ€™s minds. This process feels awkward. Asking coworkers out of the blue, â€œTell me a story about hope,â€ is a bit weird. Itâ€™s better if you lay some groundwork. Give them a reasonable basis for the question. For example, say, â€œIâ€™m trying to get a fix on how we approach projects, and I need your help. This doesnâ€™t have to apply to your job, but how would you characterize our willingness to risk in pursuit of extraordinary outcomes?â€ Be prepared to stay silent when they give you strange looks. Steer them away from hypothetical or existential discussions. Keep asking for a concrete example. Ask them for a story. If necessary, tell your own story about a recent or important experience of hope or hopelessness. Donâ€™t force your story or theirs to move in the direction you would preferâ€”that will give you a false reading. Donâ€™t try to craft the story in any way. This is a process of mapping the mental terrain. You donâ€™t draw maps based on where you wish the mountains were. Useful maps show where the mountains actually are. ([LocationÂ 2227](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2227)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522284}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Holding your story at bay while you fully experience othersâ€™ points of view without judgments is the key to real listening. ([LocationÂ 2270](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2270)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522285}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our world moves so fast that we are pressured to make sense of incoming data as quickly as possible. Speed and focus have become unquestioned values applied to communication. People mindlessly try to improve communication by increasing speed and focus. Thatâ€™s great when you seek only to inform. However, when you seek to influence, speed and focus narrow the bandwidth across which you communicate. Narrow connections produce less influence. Listening is slow and wide rather than fast and narrow. ([LocationÂ 2271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522286}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Listening is not something you do all the time or without end. It is a choice based on what you can process and how important it is for you to make a connection. Itâ€™s not like handing the keys to your brain over to a stranger. You are still in charge. True, genuine listening feels risky when time is short, but you donâ€™t abdicate decision making just because you slow down long enough to imagine another personâ€™s experiences. Listening may take you to a place you donâ€™t want to go. Listening may reveal trouble about which you were blissfully ignorant. ([LocationÂ 2301](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2301)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522287}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you donâ€™t have a high tolerance for imperfection, people will shy away from telling you the â€œwhole story.â€ And itâ€™s common wisdom that the rest of the story is where you find the most interesting bits. { #ref-552522288}

##### Choose the Stories You Tell
>[!QUOTE]  
>As mentioned before, if we were to judge people by the stories they tell on a daily basis, we would conclude most of them are stressed-out, misunderstood victims (Who-I-Am) here to survive red tape and stupid decisions (Why-I-Am-Here). They pine for retirement or the firing of a certain individual (Vision), and they believe that the â€œhavesâ€ couldnâ€™t care less about the â€œhave-notsâ€ (Value-in-Action). ([LocationÂ 338](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=338)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-538555099}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>When, during this typical up-and-down day, does your team experience you? When you are happy, productive, and at your best? Or when you are disappointed and frustrated? Most of us have to admit we are not that noticeable when we are happy and productive. We sort of fade into the woodwork. The times we seek attention are the times when we think a correction needs to be made. Indeed, Western culture is almost addicted to correction. ([LocationÂ 362](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=362)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-538555100}


---
>[!QUOTE]

#### Teaching Stories
>[!QUOTE]

#### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Storytelling brings substance to any vision process. { #ref-542655689}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our purpose here is to build a future story that pulls us in. A â€œpullâ€ story conjures positive emotions such as desire, hope, belonging, or happiness. ([LocationÂ 1228](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1228)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655690}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Stories based on negative emotions such as fear (greed is just another word for fear) fuel stress (also a word for fear) and feed perceptions of danger, scarcity, and us/them thinking. { #ref-542655691}

{ #ref-542655692}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? #### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. These loops cycle endlessly as new ideas are met with a â€œyes, butâ€ rejection followed by sympathy, which elicits more examples of feeling trapped, which trigger new ideas for change, and you are back to â€œyes, but.â€ Itâ€™s an infinite loop that you can focus like a laser once you are willing to choose one value over another. ([LocationÂ 1908](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1908)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522266}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The breadth and depth of your investment shines through your story with brightness, intensity, and essence. Itâ€™s like using a 100-watt bulb instead of a 30-watt bulb. Each bulb is the same size, turned on for the same three minutes, but one is more intense and its light reaches farther than the other. ([LocationÂ 1935](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1935)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522267}


---
>[!QUOTE]

([LocationÂ 1942](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1942)) #âœ‚ï¸

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Thatâ€™s brevity at its best. Take your longest story and ask yourself, â€œWhat is the central message I want to communicate?â€ ([LocationÂ 1953](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1953)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522269}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you were going to give a one-hour presentation on your most important project and its relevance to the organizationâ€™s mission, what would it look like? Notice your mental to-do list. { #ref-552522270}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Brevity is better achieved by turning the computer off and asking yourself the basic questions. Who am I? Why am I here? What is the highest possible outcome of my presentation? { #ref-552522271}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Graphs tidy up reality, whereas stories reveal and help resolve the mess. Avoiding the messiness of reality is not the only way to be brief. Taking a stand achieves clarity, brevity, and the power of a clear, clean message. It requires that you both respect and trust your audience. And the minute you make that decision, it rearranges your presentation into a story that sticks, and it helps you edit your graphs and numbers down to the vital few. #### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. Retaining the ambiguity and then testing a story in a low-risk experiment is a much better way to minimize risk in storytelling. ([LocationÂ 2048](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2048)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522275}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. Groups in the unfamiliar territory of images, metaphor, and emotion often seek relief by oversimplifying these immeasurable qualities and converting them into some kind of quantifiable criteria. This default to numbers doesnâ€™t make for a better story; it just allows for an easier decision. ([LocationÂ 2053](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2053)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. If a middle manager gives a report to senior management, secretly thinking they are a bunch of idiots and that his or her presentation will be futile, chances are the presentation will be futile. These nonverbal communications may never reach the presenterâ€™s or senior managementâ€™s awareness, but negative feelings distort interpretations. ([LocationÂ 2152](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2152)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522279}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. ---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In the same way that no person can experience life without bias, no story is without bias. A story without bias is boring because it becomes disembodied, sterilized, and dehumanized. ([LocationÂ 2166](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2166)) #âœ‚ï¸

>[!QUOTE]  
>Point of view alters meaning, but, more important to you, point of view also creates meaning. #### Story Listening
>[!QUOTE]  
>Before you make another presentation about a future project, I recommend you stop and listen to the stories that are operating right now in your mind and in your audienceâ€™s minds. This process feels awkward. Asking coworkers out of the blue, â€œTell me a story about hope,â€ is a bit weird. Itâ€™s better if you lay some groundwork. Give them a reasonable basis for the question. For example, say, â€œIâ€™m trying to get a fix on how we approach projects, and I need your help. This doesnâ€™t have to apply to your job, but how would you characterize our willingness to risk in pursuit of extraordinary outcomes?â€ Be prepared to stay silent when they give you strange looks. Steer them away from hypothetical or existential discussions. Keep asking for a concrete example. Ask them for a story. If necessary, tell your own story about a recent or important experience of hope or hopelessness. Donâ€™t force your story or theirs to move in the direction you would preferâ€”that will give you a false reading. Donâ€™t try to craft the story in any way. This is a process of mapping the mental terrain. You donâ€™t draw maps based on where you wish the mountains were. Useful maps show where the mountains actually are. ([LocationÂ 2227](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2227)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522284}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Holding your story at bay while you fully experience othersâ€™ points of view without judgments is the key to real listening. ([LocationÂ 2270](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2270)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522285}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our world moves so fast that we are pressured to make sense of incoming data as quickly as possible. Speed and focus have become unquestioned values applied to communication. People mindlessly try to improve communication by increasing speed and focus. Thatâ€™s great when you seek only to inform. However, when you seek to influence, speed and focus narrow the bandwidth across which you communicate. Narrow connections produce less influence. Listening is slow and wide rather than fast and narrow. ([LocationÂ 2271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522286}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Listening is not something you do all the time or without end. It is a choice based on what you can process and how important it is for you to make a connection. Itâ€™s not like handing the keys to your brain over to a stranger. You are still in charge. True, genuine listening feels risky when time is short, but you donâ€™t abdicate decision making just because you slow down long enough to imagine another personâ€™s experiences. Listening may take you to a place you donâ€™t want to go. Listening may reveal trouble about which you were blissfully ignorant. Still, listening always makes you smarter and earns you stronger connections to work with and through. ([LocationÂ 2301](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2301)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522287}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you donâ€™t have a high tolerance for imperfection, people will shy away from telling you the â€œwhole story.â€ And itâ€™s common wisdom that the rest of the story is where you find the most interesting bits. { #ref-552522288}

>Borrowing Genius ([LocationÂ 2327](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2327)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Storytelling brings substance to any vision process. { #ref-542655689}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our purpose here is to build a future story that pulls us in. A â€œpullâ€ story conjures positive emotions such as desire, hope, belonging, or happiness. ([LocationÂ 1228](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1228)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655690}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Stories based on negative emotions such as fear (greed is just another word for fear) fuel stress (also a word for fear) and feed perceptions of danger, scarcity, and us/them thinking. { #ref-542655691}


---

{ #ref-542655692}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? #### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. #### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. >[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. { #ref-552522276}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In her book On Becoming an Artist, Ellen Langer cites research into the â€œillusion of controlâ€ as she ruthlessly deconstructs our favorite habits of using set principles and cognitive frames to improve decisions.9 Particularly in business, routines that make decisions easier may sacrifice the engaging messiness of human experience. The better we get at acting like automatons, the less meaningful our stories become. ([LocationÂ 2062](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2062)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522277}

#### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. If a middle manager gives a report to senior management, secretly thinking they are a bunch of idiots and that his or her presentation will be futile, chances are the presentation will be futile. These nonverbal communications may never reach the presenterâ€™s or senior managementâ€™s awareness, but negative feelings distort interpretations. ([LocationÂ 2152](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2152)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522279}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. Senior managers may think some workers join unions to exploit that power and compensate for failed careers. School teachers think the principal is really a pawn. The CEO thinks board members are already biased. There are a thousand stories out there that leave you feeling â€œrightâ€ but keep you from being connected. ([LocationÂ 2155](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2155)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522280}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In the same way that no person can experience life without bias, no story is without bias. A story without bias is boring because it becomes disembodied, sterilized, and dehumanized. Behavior is always based on some point of view, so own it and demonstrate that you arenâ€™t blind to (or blinded by) your personal bias. ([LocationÂ 2166](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2166)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522281}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Point of view alters meaning, but, more important to you, point of view also creates meaning. Choosing a time, place, and point of view from which to tell a story is what makes a story feel like a real experience. Using your story to travel through time and space can demonstrate how many equally valid points of view you see. ([LocationÂ 2173](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2173)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522282}

#### Story Listening
>[!QUOTE]  
>Before you make another presentation about a future project, I recommend you stop and listen to the stories that are operating right now in your mind and in your audienceâ€™s minds. This process feels awkward. Asking coworkers out of the blue, â€œTell me a story about hope,â€ is a bit weird. Itâ€™s better if you lay some groundwork. Give them a reasonable basis for the question. For example, say, â€œIâ€™m trying to get a fix on how we approach projects, and I need your help. This doesnâ€™t have to apply to your job, but how would you characterize our willingness to risk in pursuit of extraordinary outcomes?â€ Be prepared to stay silent when they give you strange looks. Steer them away from hypothetical or existential discussions. Keep asking for a concrete example. Ask them for a story. If necessary, tell your own story about a recent or important experience of hope or hopelessness. Donâ€™t force your story or theirs to move in the direction you would preferâ€”that will give you a false reading. Donâ€™t try to craft the story in any way. This is a process of mapping the mental terrain. You donâ€™t draw maps based on where you wish the mountains were. Useful maps show where the mountains actually are. ([LocationÂ 2227](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2227)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522284}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Holding your story at bay while you fully experience othersâ€™ points of view without judgments is the key to real listening. ([LocationÂ 2270](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2270)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522285}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our world moves so fast that we are pressured to make sense of incoming data as quickly as possible. Speed and focus have become unquestioned values applied to communication. People mindlessly try to improve communication by increasing speed and focus. Thatâ€™s great when you seek only to inform. However, when you seek to influence, speed and focus narrow the bandwidth across which you communicate. Narrow connections produce less influence. Listening is slow and wide rather than fast and narrow. ([LocationÂ 2271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522286}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Listening is not something you do all the time or without end. It is a choice based on what you can process and how important it is for you to make a connection. Itâ€™s not like handing the keys to your brain over to a stranger. You are still in charge. True, genuine listening feels risky when time is short, but you donâ€™t abdicate decision making just because you slow down long enough to imagine another personâ€™s experiences. Listening may take you to a place you donâ€™t want to go. Listening may reveal trouble about which you were blissfully ignorant. Still, listening always makes you smarter and earns you stronger connections to work with and through. ([LocationÂ 2301](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2301)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522287}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you donâ€™t have a high tolerance for imperfection, people will shy away from telling you the â€œwhole story.â€ And itâ€™s common wisdom that the rest of the story is where you find the most interesting bits. One time, I heard someone complain that her story was boring, so I automatically replied, â€œThen you need to tell the truth.â€ She responded, â€œDo you mean the part when I had an affair with the minister?â€ That certainly didnâ€™t sound very boring to me. ([LocationÂ 2317](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2317)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522288}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Borrowing Genius ([LocationÂ 2327](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2327)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522289}

>[!QUOTE]  
>Design engineers also use templates to describe requirements in the terms of a user story: â€œAs a (type of user) I want (goal) so that (reason why).â€ While these user stories are currently very technical, notice that this template actually asks for three of the six stories addressed in this book, â€œWho are you? #### Teaching Stories
>[!QUOTE]

#### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]

{ #ref-542655689}

---
>[!QUOTE]

>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? #### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. These loops cycle endlessly as new ideas are met with a â€œyes, butâ€ rejection followed by sympathy, which elicits more examples of feeling trapped, which trigger new ideas for change, and you are back to â€œyes, but.â€ Itâ€™s an infinite loop that you can focus like a laser once you are willing to choose one value over another. #### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. ([LocationÂ 2048](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2048)) #âœ‚ï¸

>[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. #### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. If a middle manager gives a report to senior management, secretly thinking they are a bunch of idiots and that his or her presentation will be futile, chances are the presentation will be futile. These nonverbal communications may never reach the presenterâ€™s or senior managementâ€™s awareness, but negative feelings distort interpretations. ([LocationÂ 2152](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2152)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522279}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. Senior managers may think some workers join unions to exploit that power and compensate for failed careers. School teachers think the principal is really a pawn. The CEO thinks board members are already biased. There are a thousand stories out there that leave you feeling â€œrightâ€ but keep you from being connected. ([LocationÂ 2155](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2155)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522280}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In the same way that no person can experience life without bias, no story is without bias. A story without bias is boring because it becomes disembodied, sterilized, and dehumanized. Behavior is always based on some point of view, so own it and demonstrate that you arenâ€™t blind to (or blinded by) your personal bias. ([LocationÂ 2166](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2166)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522281}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Point of view alters meaning, but, more important to you, point of view also creates meaning. #### Story Listening
>[!QUOTE]  
>Before you make another presentation about a future project, I recommend you stop and listen to the stories that are operating right now in your mind and in your audienceâ€™s minds. This process feels awkward. Asking coworkers out of the blue, â€œTell me a story about hope,â€ is a bit weird. Itâ€™s better if you lay some groundwork. Give them a reasonable basis for the question. For example, say, â€œIâ€™m trying to get a fix on how we approach projects, and I need your help. This doesnâ€™t have to apply to your job, but how would you characterize our willingness to risk in pursuit of extraordinary outcomes?â€ Be prepared to stay silent when they give you strange looks. Steer them away from hypothetical or existential discussions. Keep asking for a concrete example. Ask them for a story. If necessary, tell your own story about a recent or important experience of hope or hopelessness. Donâ€™t force your story or theirs to move in the direction you would preferâ€”that will give you a false reading. Donâ€™t try to craft the story in any way. This is a process of mapping the mental terrain. You donâ€™t draw maps based on where you wish the mountains were. Useful maps show where the mountains actually are. ([LocationÂ 2227](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2227)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522284}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Holding your story at bay while you fully experience othersâ€™ points of view without judgments is the key to real listening. ([LocationÂ 2270](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2270)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522285}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our world moves so fast that we are pressured to make sense of incoming data as quickly as possible. Speed and focus have become unquestioned values applied to communication. People mindlessly try to improve communication by increasing speed and focus. Thatâ€™s great when you seek only to inform. However, when you seek to influence, speed and focus narrow the bandwidth across which you communicate. Narrow connections produce less influence. Listening is slow and wide rather than fast and narrow. ([LocationÂ 2271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522286}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Listening is not something you do all the time or without end. It is a choice based on what you can process and how important it is for you to make a connection. Itâ€™s not like handing the keys to your brain over to a stranger. You are still in charge. True, genuine listening feels risky when time is short, but you donâ€™t abdicate decision making just because you slow down long enough to imagine another personâ€™s experiences. { #ref-539068637}

>[!QUOTE]  
>Often, those we need to influence donâ€™t know us very well, so they canâ€™t trust us. A good Who-I-Am story is like increasing the bandwidth between you and your listeners, thus increasing the amount of information your listeners are ready to receive. ([LocationÂ 755](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=755)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-539068638}

#### Why-I-Am-Here Stories
##### Telling Others Whatâ€™s in It for You
>[!QUOTE]  
>However, Iâ€™ve noticed (and you may also have noticed) that people donâ€™t relax and listen to what is in it for them until they are satisfied they know whatâ€™s in it for you. ([LocationÂ 864](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=864)) #âœ‚ï¸

>[!QUOTE]  
>Simulations in experimental economics, such as investment games and public good games, reveal that fairness and reciprocity often matter more than utility. ##### Exercise: Why You Are Here
#### Teaching Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>AT ITS BEST, a Teaching story transports listeners into an experience that empowers them to emotionally feel as well as hear, see, taste, touch, and smell an excellent performance or the unforgettable consequences of a poor performance. It demonstrates how new behaviors create new results. It is a no-risk demonstration: a trial by imagination. ([LocationÂ 1034](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1034)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655685}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>One caveat for using stories to teach: remember, it only works about 70 percent of the time. When you inspire and encourage people to think for themselves, they are more actively engaged and pay better attention. However, at times, their interpretation will not be what you expected. Thatâ€™s the price you pay for creative engagement. ([LocationÂ 1076](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1076)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655686}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Find people who have the skills you want. Study their stories, and tell their stories. We tend to teach what we need to learn, and I canâ€™t think of a better way to do it. ([LocationÂ 1157](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1157)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655687}

#### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Storytelling brings substance to any vision process. When you apply the discipline of interpreting your vision with a story, the process often exposes â€œplot holesâ€ that may send you back to the drawing board. Building a Vision story is a good way to run thought experiments that can anticipate contrary perceptions or unintended outcomes. ([LocationÂ 1207](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1207)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655689}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our purpose here is to build a future story that pulls us in. A â€œpullâ€ story conjures positive emotions such as desire, hope, belonging, or happiness. ([LocationÂ 1228](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1228)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655690}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Stories based on negative emotions such as fear (greed is just another word for fear) fuel stress (also a word for fear) and feed perceptions of danger, scarcity, and us/them thinking. Fear is a physiological and psychological state that narrows vision and limits creativity. ([LocationÂ 1229](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1229)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655691}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The molecules of fear sacrifice peripheral vision in favor of focusing on problems and our three basic human responses: fight, flight, or freeze. Fear can make you stupid. It compartmentalizes every IQ point you have into closed loops of worst-case scenarios. ([LocationÂ 1231](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1231)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655692}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? Start by imagining a time 5 to 10 years from now when you have just achieved a set of tangible goals (experiment with a few or conduct a vision trip for each goal one at a time). Let your imagination observe a typical day in tangible detail. You may have to wait, but your imagination will do its job if you are patient. ([LocationÂ 1239](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1239)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655693}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>You can illustrate your Vision story like a comic strip or find an analogy that is similar to the achievements, obstacles, and events you imagined. ([LocationÂ 1245](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1245)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655694}

>>[!NOTE]  
>>Write fiction, past tense as if itd already happened

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story carved in stone is not as good as one that is loose enough to adapt and change along the way. ([LocationÂ 1271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655695}

([LocationÂ 1338](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1338)) #âœ‚ï¸

---
>[!QUOTE]

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Like many values, for instance, trust is not strictly rational. Trust means I can fall and you wonâ€™t leave me. ([LocationÂ 1438](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1438)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. These loops cycle endlessly as new ideas are met with a â€œyes, butâ€ rejection followed by sympathy, which elicits more examples of feeling trapped, which trigger new ideas for change, and you are back to â€œyes, but.â€ Itâ€™s an infinite loop that you can focus like a laser once you are willing to choose one value over another. ([LocationÂ 1908](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1908)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522266}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The breadth and depth of your investment shines through your story with brightness, intensity, and essence. Itâ€™s like using a 100-watt bulb instead of a 30-watt bulb. Each bulb is the same size, turned on for the same three minutes, but one is more intense and its light reaches farther than the other. ([LocationÂ 1935](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1935)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522267}

([LocationÂ 1942](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1942)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522268}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Thatâ€™s brevity at its best. Take your longest story and ask yourself, â€œWhat is the central message I want to communicate?â€ ([LocationÂ 1953](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1953)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522269}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you were going to give a one-hour presentation on your most important project and its relevance to the organizationâ€™s mission, what would it look like? Notice your mental to-do list. { #ref-552522270}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Brevity is better achieved by turning the computer off and asking yourself the basic questions. Who am I? Why am I here? What is the highest possible outcome of my presentation? { #ref-552522271}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Graphs tidy up reality, whereas stories reveal and help resolve the mess. Avoiding the messiness of reality is not the only way to be brief. Taking a stand achieves clarity, brevity, and the power of a clear, clean message. It requires that you both respect and trust your audience. And the minute you make that decision, it rearranges your presentation into a story that sticks, and it helps you edit your graphs and numbers down to the vital few. { #ref-552522272}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Core values always compete at some point. It takes courage to make the call, in advance, about which value you choose at the extremes when two values conflict. ([LocationÂ 1996](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1996)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. Retaining the ambiguity and then testing a story in a low-risk experiment is a much better way to minimize risk in storytelling. ([LocationÂ 2048](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2048)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522275}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. Groups in the unfamiliar territory of images, metaphor, and emotion often seek relief by oversimplifying these immeasurable qualities and converting them into some kind of quantifiable criteria. This default to numbers doesnâ€™t make for a better story; it just allows for an easier decision. ([LocationÂ 2053](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2053)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522276}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In her book On Becoming an Artist, Ellen Langer cites research into the â€œillusion of controlâ€ as she ruthlessly deconstructs our favorite habits of using set principles and cognitive frames to improve decisions.9 Particularly in business, routines that make decisions easier may sacrifice the engaging messiness of human experience. The better we get at acting like automatons, the less meaningful our stories become. ([LocationÂ 2062](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2062)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. If a middle manager gives a report to senior management, secretly thinking they are a bunch of idiots and that his or her presentation will be futile, chances are the presentation will be futile. These nonverbal communications may never reach the presenterâ€™s or senior managementâ€™s awareness, but negative feelings distort interpretations. ([LocationÂ 2152](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2152)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522279}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. Senior managers may think some workers join unions to exploit that power and compensate for failed careers. School teachers think the principal is really a pawn. The CEO thinks board members are already biased. There are a thousand stories out there that leave you feeling â€œrightâ€ but keep you from being connected. ([LocationÂ 2155](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2155)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522280}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In the same way that no person can experience life without bias, no story is without bias. A story without bias is boring because it becomes disembodied, sterilized, and dehumanized. Behavior is always based on some point of view, so own it and demonstrate that you arenâ€™t blind to (or blinded by) your personal bias. ([LocationÂ 2166](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2166)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522281}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Point of view alters meaning, but, more important to you, point of view also creates meaning. Choosing a time, place, and point of view from which to tell a story is what makes a story feel like a real experience. Using your story to travel through time and space can demonstrate how many equally valid points of view you see. ([LocationÂ 2173](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2173)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522282}

#### Story Listening
>[!QUOTE]  
>Before you make another presentation about a future project, I recommend you stop and listen to the stories that are operating right now in your mind and in your audienceâ€™s minds. This process feels awkward. Asking coworkers out of the blue, â€œTell me a story about hope,â€ is a bit weird. Itâ€™s better if you lay some groundwork. Give them a reasonable basis for the question. For example, say, â€œIâ€™m trying to get a fix on how we approach projects, and I need your help. This doesnâ€™t have to apply to your job, but how would you characterize our willingness to risk in pursuit of extraordinary outcomes?â€ Be prepared to stay silent when they give you strange looks. Steer them away from hypothetical or existential discussions. Keep asking for a concrete example. Ask them for a story. If necessary, tell your own story about a recent or important experience of hope or hopelessness. Donâ€™t force your story or theirs to move in the direction you would preferâ€”that will give you a false reading. Donâ€™t try to craft the story in any way. This is a process of mapping the mental terrain. You donâ€™t draw maps based on where you wish the mountains were. Useful maps show where the mountains actually are. ([LocationÂ 2227](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2227)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522284}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Holding your story at bay while you fully experience othersâ€™ points of view without judgments is the key to real listening. ([LocationÂ 2270](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2270)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522285}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our world moves so fast that we are pressured to make sense of incoming data as quickly as possible. Speed and focus have become unquestioned values applied to communication. People mindlessly try to improve communication by increasing speed and focus. Thatâ€™s great when you seek only to inform. However, when you seek to influence, speed and focus narrow the bandwidth across which you communicate. Narrow connections produce less influence. Listening is slow and wide rather than fast and narrow. ([LocationÂ 2271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522286}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Listening is not something you do all the time or without end. It is a choice based on what you can process and how important it is for you to make a connection. Itâ€™s not like handing the keys to your brain over to a stranger. You are still in charge. True, genuine listening feels risky when time is short, but you donâ€™t abdicate decision making just because you slow down long enough to imagine another personâ€™s experiences. Listening may take you to a place you donâ€™t want to go. Listening may reveal trouble about which you were blissfully ignorant. Still, listening always makes you smarter and earns you stronger connections to work with and through. ([LocationÂ 2301](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2301)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522287}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>If you donâ€™t have a high tolerance for imperfection, people will shy away from telling you the â€œwhole story.â€ And itâ€™s common wisdom that the rest of the story is where you find the most interesting bits. One time, I heard someone complain that her story was boring, so I automatically replied, â€œThen you need to tell the truth.â€ She responded, â€œDo you mean the part when I had an affair with the minister?â€ That certainly didnâ€™t sound very boring to me. ([LocationÂ 2317](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2317)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522288}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Borrowing Genius ([LocationÂ 2327](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2327)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522289}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Design engineers also use templates to describe requirements in the terms of a user story: â€œAs a (type of user) I want (goal) so that (reason why).â€ While these user stories are currently very technical, notice that this template actually asks for three of the six stories addressed in this book, â€œWho are you? #### Vision Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story builds a sensory, imagined future, just like a bright, shiny bicycle in a store window that motivates a child to do more chores. Stimulating imagined experiences of that future bicycle fuels energy to rummage through trash for aluminum cans, to babysit little monsters, or even to wash the family car. After we see, taste, touch, smell, and hear a realistic and emotionally compelling future in our imagination, work seems less menial and difficult in relation to the payoff. ([LocationÂ 1203](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1203)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655688}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Storytelling brings substance to any vision process. Building a Vision story is a good way to run thought experiments that can anticipate contrary perceptions or unintended outcomes. ([LocationÂ 1207](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1207)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655689}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Our purpose here is to build a future story that pulls us in. A â€œpullâ€ story conjures positive emotions such as desire, hope, belonging, or happiness. ([LocationÂ 1228](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1228)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655690}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Stories based on negative emotions such as fear (greed is just another word for fear) fuel stress (also a word for fear) and feed perceptions of danger, scarcity, and us/them thinking. Fear is a physiological and psychological state that narrows vision and limits creativity. ([LocationÂ 1229](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1229)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655691}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The molecules of fear sacrifice peripheral vision in favor of focusing on problems and our three basic human responses: fight, flight, or freeze. Fear can make you stupid. It compartmentalizes every IQ point you have into closed loops of worst-case scenarios. ([LocationÂ 1231](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1231)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655692}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>What is your vision? Start by imagining a time 5 to 10 years from now when you have just achieved a set of tangible goals (experiment with a few or conduct a vision trip for each goal one at a time). Let your imagination observe a typical day in tangible detail. You may have to wait, but your imagination will do its job if you are patient. ([LocationÂ 1239](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1239)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655693}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>You can illustrate your Vision story like a comic strip or find an analogy that is similar to the achievements, obstacles, and events you imagined. ([LocationÂ 1245](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1245)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655694}

>>[!NOTE]  
>>Write fiction, past tense as if itd already happened

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>A Vision story carved in stone is not as good as one that is loose enough to adapt and change along the way. ([LocationÂ 1271](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1271)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655695}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>One final note: In my humble opinion, fiction is a dangerous place to go hunting for Vision stories because even great fiction is an invented world that may have been idealized and was certainly manipulated to grab and keep attention. The benefit of true stories is that they actually happened (at least once), so it seems more likely that you could make them happen again. ([LocationÂ 1338](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1338)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-542655696}

#### Value-in-Action Stories
>[!QUOTE]

{ #ref-552522250}


---

{ #ref-552522251}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Metaphors are ministories that help us frame complexity into a familiar package. We use the war metaphor a lot: the war on AIDS, for instance. ([LocationÂ 1369](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1369)) #âœ‚ï¸

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Each culture prizes different values. Mix two cultures and you better start sharing Value-in-Action stories. ---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Like many values, for instance, trust is not strictly rational. Trust means I can fall and you wonâ€™t leave me. ([LocationÂ 1438](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1438)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### I-Know-What-You-Are-Thinking Stories
>[!QUOTE]  
>Many arguments are fueled less by the â€œneed to be rightâ€ and more by the chronically unmet need to be heard and respected. Thatâ€™s where I-Know-What-You-Are-Thinking stories come into play. #### The Gift of Brevity
>[!QUOTE]  
>What does this have to do with brevity? The ambivalence of wanting to appear sympathetic and wanting to give someone a kick in the butt can trap you in a looping conversation. These loops cycle endlessly as new ideas are met with a â€œyes, butâ€ rejection followed by sympathy, which elicits more examples of feeling trapped, which trigger new ideas for change, and you are back to â€œyes, but.â€ Itâ€™s an infinite loop that you can focus like a laser once you are willing to choose one value over another. ([LocationÂ 1908](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1908)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522266}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>The breadth and depth of your investment shines through your story with brightness, intensity, and essence. Itâ€™s like using a 100-watt bulb instead of a 30-watt bulb. ([LocationÂ 1935](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=1935)) #âœ‚ï¸

>[!QUOTE]  
>If you were going to give a one-hour presentation on your most important project and its relevance to the organizationâ€™s mission, what would it look like? Notice your mental to-do list. >[!QUOTE]

---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Graphs tidy up reality, whereas stories reveal and help resolve the mess. Avoiding the messiness of reality is not the only way to be brief. Taking a stand achieves clarity, brevity, and the power of a clear, clean message. It requires that you both respect and trust your audience. And the minute you make that decision, it rearranges your presentation into a story that sticks, and it helps you edit your graphs and numbers down to the vital few. >[!QUOTE]

#### Storytelling by Committee
>[!QUOTE]  
>Objective thinkers seem to think that reducing ambiguity is the same as reducing riskâ€”not so. Ambiguity reflects real life, so it makes a story feel real. Retaining the ambiguity and then testing a story in a low-risk experiment is a much better way to minimize risk in storytelling. ([LocationÂ 2048](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2048)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522275}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Choosing the right story depends equally on your groupâ€™s imagination and your groupâ€™s skill in making subjective decisions. Groups in the unfamiliar territory of images, metaphor, and emotion often seek relief by oversimplifying these immeasurable qualities and converting them into some kind of quantifiable criteria. This default to numbers doesnâ€™t make for a better story; it just allows for an easier decision. ([LocationÂ 2053](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2053)) #âœ‚ï¸

#### Point of View
>[!QUOTE]  
>Tone, facial expressions, and body language will always give away the story you are telling yourself about your audience. If a middle manager gives a report to senior management, secretly thinking they are a bunch of idiots and that his or her presentation will be futile, chances are the presentation will be futile. These nonverbal communications may never reach the presenterâ€™s or senior managementâ€™s awareness, but negative feelings distort interpretations. ([LocationÂ 2152](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2152)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522279}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Most presentations of vital importance are between two groups that harbor secret stories that discount or discredit the other. Senior managers may think some workers join unions to exploit that power and compensate for failed careers. School teachers think the principal is really a pawn. The CEO thinks board members are already biased. There are a thousand stories out there that leave you feeling â€œrightâ€ but keep you from being connected. { #ref-552522280}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>In the same way that no person can experience life without bias, no story is without bias. A story without bias is boring because it becomes disembodied, sterilized, and dehumanized. Behavior is always based on some point of view, so own it and demonstrate that you arenâ€™t blind to (or blinded by) your personal bias. ([LocationÂ 2166](https://readwise.io/to_kindle?action=open&asin=B00TRF2QV0&location=2166)) #âœ‚ï¸ 
{ #ref-552522281}


---
>[!QUOTE]  
>Point of view alters meaning, but, more important to you, point of view also creates meaning.
